                    }
                }

<!--Polyfills CustomEvent constructor in IE. Allows us to use events to manage race conditions in client side js-->
<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41467"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="Genome-enabled discovery of anthraquinone biosynthesis in Senna tora"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature Communications 2020 11:1"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2020-11-18"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2020 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Senna tora is a widely used medicinal plant. Its health benefits have been attributed to the large quantity of anthraquinones, but how they are made in plants remains a mystery. To identify the genes responsible for plant anthraquinone biosynthesis, we reveal the genome sequence of S. tora at the chromosome level with 526 Mb (96%) assembled into 13 chromosomes. Comparison among related plant species shows that a chalcone synthase-like (CHS-L) gene family has lineage-specifically and rapidly expanded in S. tora. Combining genomics, transcriptomics, metabolomics, and biochemistry, we identify a CHS-L gene contributing to the biosynthesis of anthraquinones. The S. tora reference genome will accelerate the discovery of biologically active anthraquinone biosynthesis pathways in medicinal plants. Anthraquinones are aromatic polyketides and have been used for treating various diseases, but the biosynthetic pathway is unclear. Here, the authors assemble the genome of an&#160;anthraquinone-producing medicinal&#160;plant Senna tora and show the evidences that CHS-like genes may be involved in anthraquinone biosynthesis."/>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p><i>Senna tora</i> is a widely used medicinal plant. Its health benefits have been attributed to the large quantity of anthraquinones, but how they are made in plants remains a mystery. To identify the genes responsible for plant anthraquinone biosynthesis, we reveal the genome sequence of <i>S. tora</i> at the chromosome level with 526 Mb (96%) assembled into 13 chromosomes. Comparison among related plant species shows that a chalcone synthase-like (CHS-L) gene family has lineage-specifically and rapidly expanded in <i>S. tora</i>. Combining genomics, transcriptomics, metabolomics, and biochemistry, we identify a CHS-L gene contributing to the biosynthesis of anthraquinones. The <i>S. tora</i> reference genome will accelerate the discovery of biologically active anthraquinone biosynthesis pathways in medicinal plants.</p></div></div></section>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Introduction"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Introduction</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p><i>Senna tora</i> (L.) Roxb., also known as <i>Cassia tora</i>, is a favorite of ancient Chinese and Ayurvedic herbal medicine that is now widely used around the world and recorded as ModelÂ List of Essential MedicinesÂ by theÂ World Health Organization<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="World Health Organization. Model List of Essential Medicines, 21st List 2019 (WHO, Geneva, 2019)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d58070e879">1</a></sup>. Recent studies point to <i>S. tora</i>âs beneficial activities against microbial<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Manoharan, R. Anti-influenza A virus activity of rhein through regulating oxidative stress, TLR4, Akt, MAPK, and NF-ÎºB signal pathways. PLoS ONE 13, e0191793 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d58070e889">5</a></sup> and parasitic<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Dhananjeyan, M. Health functions and structure-activity relationships of natural anthraquinones from plants. Food Funct. 9, 6063â6080 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d58070e898">7</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Ravi, S. Cassia tora prevents AÎ²1-42 aggregation, inhibits acetylcholinesterase activity and protects against AÎ²1-42-induced cell death and oxidative stress in human neuroblastoma cells. Pharmacol. Rep. 71, 1151â1159 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d58070e901">8</a></sup>, and diabetes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Cheng, F.-R., Cui, H.-X., Fang, J.-L., Yuan, K. &amp; Guo, Y. Ameliorative effect and mechanism of the purified anthraquinone-glycoside preparation from Rheum Palmatum L. on type 2 diabetes mellitus. Molecules 24, 1454 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d58070e905">9</a></sup>. <i>S. tora</i>âs positive health impact is attributed to the significant amount of anthraquinones in mature seeds and other parts of the plant<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Chaubey, M. &amp; Kapoor, V. Res. 332, 439â444 (2001)." href="#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d58070e912">10</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Fernand, V. E. et al. Determination of pharmacologically active compounds in root extracts of Cassia alata L. by use of high performance liquid chromatography. Talanta 74, 896â902 (2008)." href="#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d58070e912_1">11</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Mahesh, V. K., Sharma, R., Singh, R. S. &amp; Upadhya, S. K. Anthraquinones and kaempferol from Cassia species section fistula. J. Nat. Products 47, 733â733 (1984)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d58070e915">12</a></sup>. As an ancient medicine, anthraquinones from seeds of other <i>Senna</i> species are commonly used for treating various diseases<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Boy, H. I. A. et al. Recommended medicinal plants as source of natural products: a review. Digital Chin. Med. 1, 131â142 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d58070e922">13</a></sup>. Despite the extensive applications of <i>Senna</i> plants in medicine and industry<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="World Health Organization. Model List of Essential Medicines, 21st List 2019 (WHO, Geneva, 2019)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d58070e930">1</a></sup>, molecular and genomic studies of this remarkable genus of plants have been limited<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Choudhri, P. et al. De novo transcriptome sequence of Senna tora provides insights into anthraquinone biosynthesis. PLoS ONE 15, e0225564 (2020)." href="#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d58070e934_1">15</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Mehta, R. H., Ponnuchamy, M., Kumar, J. &amp; Reddy, N. R. R. Exploring drought stress-regulated genes in senna (Cassia angustifolia Vahl.): a transcriptomic approach. Funct. Integr. Genomics 17, 1â25 (2017)." href="#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d58070e934_2">16</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Deng, Y. et al. Full-length transcriptome survey and expression analysis of Cassia obtusifolia to discover putative genes related to aurantio-obtusin biosynthesis, seed formation and development, and stress response. Int. J. Mol. Sci. 19, 2476 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d58070e937">17</a></sup>. Elucidating the genes responsible for the biosynthesis of anthraquinones in <i>S. tora</i> will aid molecular breeding and the development of tools for probing its biochemistry.</p><p>Anthraquinones are aromatic polyketides made by bacteria, fungi, insects, and plants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Chiang, Y.-M. et al. Characterization of the Aspergillus nidulans monodictyphenone gene cluster. Appl. Environ. Microbiol. 76, 2067â2074 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d58070e947">18</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Shamim, G., Ranjan, K. S., Pandey, M. D. &amp; Ramani, R. Biochemistry and biosynthesis of insect pigments. Eur. J. Entomol. 111, 149â164 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d58070e950">19</a></sup>. Besides their medicinal benefits, natural anthraquinones are garnering attention as alternatives to synthetic dyes that damage aquatic ecosystems<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Duval, J., Pecher, V., Poujol, M. &amp; Lesellier, E. Research advances for the extraction, analysis and uses of anthraquinones: a review. Ind. Crops Products 94, 812â833 (2016)." href="#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d58070e954">20</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Javaid, R. &amp; Qazi, U. Y. Catalytic oxidation process for the degradation of synthetic dyes: an overview. Int. J. Environ. Res. Public Health 16, 2066 (2019)." href="#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d58070e954_1">21</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Tkaczyk, A., Mitrowska, K. &amp; Posyniak, A. Synthetic organic dyes as contaminants of the aquatic environment and their implications for ecosystems: a review. Sci. Total Environ. 717, 137222 (2020)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d58070e957">22</a></sup>. Bacteria, fungi, and insects make anthraquinones via a polyketide pathway usingÂ complexÂ  polyketide synthaseÂ enzymes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Chiang, Y.-M. et al. Characterization of the Aspergillus nidulans monodictyphenone gene cluster. Appl. Environ. Microbiol. 76, 2067â2074 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d58070e961">18</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Awakawa, T. et al. Physically discrete Î²-lactamase-type thioesterase catalyzes product release in atrochrysone synthesis by iterative type I polyketide synthase. Chem. Biol. 16, 613â623 (2009)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d58070e964">23</a></sup>.</p><p>For plants, how anthraquinones are made remains unknown. Two biosynthesis pathways have been proposed for anthraquinones in plants: (1) a polyketide pathway<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Van Den Berg, A. J. J. &amp; Labadie, R. P. in Methods in Plant BiochemistryÂ Vol. 1 (ed. Harborne, J. B.) 451â491 (Academic, 1989)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d58070e971">24</a></sup> and (2) a combination of shikimate and mevalonate/methyl-D-erythritol 4-phosphate pathways<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Leistner, E. in Biosynthesis of Chorismate-Derived Quinones in Plant Cell Cultures (eds Neumann, K. H., Barz, W. &amp; Reinhard, E.) 215â224 (Springer, 1985)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d58070e975">25</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Leistner, E. &amp; Zenk, M. H. Mevalonic acid a precursor of the substituted benzenoid ring of rubiaceae-anthraquinones. Tetrahedron Lett. 9, 1395â1396 (1968)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d58070e978">26</a></sup>. More than three decades ago, radiolabeled feeding experiments indicated that the A and B rings of anthraquinones were derived from shikimate and <i>Î±</i>-ketoglutarate via <i>O</i>-succinylbenzoate<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Bauch, H. J. &amp; Leistner, E. Aromatic metabolites in cell suspension cultures of Galium mollugo L. Planta Med. 33, 105â123 (1978)." href="#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d58070e988">27</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Leistner, E. Isolation, identification and biosynthesis of anthraquinones in cell suspension cultures of Morinda citrifolia (authorâs transl). Planta Med.Â 28, 214â224 (1975)." href="#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d58070e988_1">28</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Leistner, E. in Medicinal and Aromatic Plants VIII. Biotechnology in Agriculture and Forestry Vol. 33 (ed. Bajaj, Y. P. S.) 296â307 (Springer, 1995)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d58070e991">29</a></sup> and C ring from mevalonate pathway via isopentenyl pyrophosphate (IPP) and dimethylallyl pyrophosphate (DMAPP)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Leistner, E. in Biosynthesis of Chorismate-Derived Quinones in Plant Cell Cultures (eds Neumann, K. H., Barz, W. &amp; Reinhard, E.) 215â224 (Springer, 1985)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d58070e996">25</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Leistner, E. &amp; Zenk, M. H. Mevalonic acid a precursor of the substituted benzenoid ring of rubiaceae-anthraquinones. Tetrahedron Lett. 9, 1395â1396 (1968)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d58070e999">26</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Burnett, A. R. &amp; Thomson, R. H. Naturally occurring quinones. Part XV. Biogenesis of the anthraquinones in Rubia tinctorum L. (madder). J. Chem. Soc. C 1968, 2437â2441 (1968)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d58070e1002">30</a></sup> or 2-C-methyl-D-erythritol 4-phosphate (MEP) pathway<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Furumoto, T. &amp; Hoshikuma, A. Biosynthetic origin of 2-geranyl-1,4-naphthoquinone and its related anthraquinone in a Sesamum indicum hairy root culture. Phytochemistry 72, 871â874 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d58070e1006">31</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Han, Y.-S., Heijden, R. V. D., Lefeber, A. W. M., Erkelens, C. &amp; Verpoorte, R. Biosynthesis of anthraquinones in cell cultures of Cinchona âRobustaâ proceeds via the methylerythritol 4-phosphate pathway. Phytochemistry 59, 45â55 (2002)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d58070e1009">32</a></sup>. Contrarily, recent studies speculated biosynthesis of anthraquinones in plants to occur via a polyketide pathway<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Abdel-Rahman, I. A. M. et al. In vitro formation of the anthranoid scaffold by cell-free extracts from yeast-extract-treated Cassia bicapsularis cell cultures. Phytochemistry 88, 15â24 (2013)." href="#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d58070e1013">33</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Karppinen, K., Hokkanen, J., Mattila, S., Neubauer, P. &amp; Hohtola, A. Octaketide-producing type III polyketide synthase from Hypericum perforatum is expressed in dark glands accumulating hypericins. FEBS J. 275, 4329â4342 (2008)." href="#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d58070e1013_1">34</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Mizuuchi, Y. et al. Novel type III polyketide synthases from Aloe arborescens. FEBS J. 276, 2391â2401 (2009)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d58070e1016">35</a></sup>. Type III polyketide synthase (PKS) enzymes could actively catalyze seven successive decarboxylative condensations of malonyl-CoA to produce anÂ octaketide chain<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Karppinen, K., Hokkanen, J., Mattila, S., Neubauer, P. &amp; Hohtola, A. Octaketide-producing type III polyketide synthase from Hypericum perforatum is expressed in dark glands accumulating hypericins. FEBS J. 275, 4329â4342 (2008)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d58070e1020">34</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Mizuuchi, Y. et al. Novel type III polyketide synthases from Aloe arborescens. FEBS J. 276, 2391â2401 (2009)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d58070e1023">35</a></sup>. The linear polyketide chain undergoes cyclization and decarboxylation reactions to produce the core unit of polyketides such as atrochrysone carboxylic acid followed by decarboxylation to atrochrysone and dehydration to emodin anthrone<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Abdel-Rahman, I. A. M. et al. In vitro formation of the anthranoid scaffold by cell-free extracts from yeast-extract-treated Cassia bicapsularis cell cultures. Phytochemistry 88, 15â24 (2013)." href="#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d58070e1027">33</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Karppinen, K., Hokkanen, J., Mattila, S., Neubauer, P. &amp; Hohtola, A. Octaketide-producing type III polyketide synthase from Hypericum perforatum is expressed in dark glands accumulating hypericins. FEBS J. 275, 4329â4342 (2008)." href="#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d58070e1027_1">34</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Mizuuchi, Y. et al. Novel type III polyketide synthases from Aloe arborescens. FEBS J. 276, 2391â2401 (2009)." href="#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d58070e1027_2">35</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Abe, I., Oguro, S., Utsumi, Y., Sano, Y. &amp; Noguchi, H. Engineered biosynthesis of plant polyketides:âchain length control in an octaketide-producing plant type III polyketide synthase. J. Am. Chem. Soc. 127, 12709â12716 (2005)." href="#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d58070e1027_3">36</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Pillai, P. P. &amp; Nair, A. R. Hypericin biosynthesis in Hypericum hookerianum Wight and Arn: investigation on biochemical pathways using metabolite inhibitors and suppression subtractive hybridization. C. R. Biol. 337, 571â580 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d58070e1030">37</a></sup>Â (Supplementary Fig. 1). However, to date, no study in type III PKS enzymes has provided conclusive evidence on the biosynthesis of anthraquinones or the intermediate metabolites of the pathways. Beerhues and colleagues<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Abdel-Rahman, I. A. M. et al. In vitro formation of the anthranoid scaffold by cell-free extracts from yeast-extract-treated Cassia bicapsularis cell cultures. Phytochemistry 88, 15â24 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d58070e1034">33</a></sup> showed promising outcomes on the biosynthesis of an anthranoid scaffold via the polyketide pathway. The in vitro reaction using acetyl-CoA, stable carbon isotope-labeled malonyl-CoA, and cell-free extracts of <i>Cassia bicapsularis</i> cell cultures produced emodin anthrone and <i>O</i>-methylated torochrysone<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Abdel-Rahman, I. A. M. et al. In vitro formation of the anthranoid scaffold by cell-free extracts from yeast-extract-treated Cassia bicapsularis cell cultures. Phytochemistry 88, 15â24 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d58070e1045">33</a></sup>. However, this study could not discern whether a PKS was involved in the biosynthesis of the anthranoid scaffolds.</p><p>In this work, we present a high-quality reference genome of <i>S. tora</i> cultivar Myeongyun, examine the evolution of candidate gene families involved in anthraquinone biosynthesis, and identify the enzyme known to catalyze a plant anthraquinone. By combining genomic, transcriptomic, metabolomic, and biochemical approaches, we systematically screen and identify a putative gene responsible for biosynthesis of an anthraquinone scaffold in <i>S. tora</i>.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results and discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results and discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3"><i>S. tora</i> genome assembly and annotation</h3><p>We generated one of the highest-quality genomes for medicinal plants. The <i>S. tora</i> cultivar Myeongyun genome was assembled with Pacific Biosciences long-read sequencing (146.2Ã coverage) by FALCON v0.4 (Supplementary TablesÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#MOESM1">1</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#MOESM1">2</a>). To improve the quality of genome assembly, we performed error correction with Sequel data by Arrow v2.1.0 and further corrected it with 101.2Ã Illumina data using BWA and GATK. <i>S. tora</i> has an estimated genome size of ~547âMb based on <i>k</i>-mer analysis (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#MOESM1">2</a>). Through chromosome conformation capture (Hi-C) mapping, we generated 13 chromosome-scale scaffolds (hereafter called chromosomes, Chr1âChr13) totaling 502.6âMb, 95.5% of the ~526.4âMb of the assembled genome (TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#Tab1">1</a> and Supplementary Figs.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#MOESM1">3</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#MOESM1">4</a>). We evaluated the quality of assembly using Benchmarking Universal Single Copy Orthologs (BUSCO)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="SimÃ£o, F. A., Waterhouse, R. M., Ioannidis, P., Kriventseva, E. V. &amp; Zdobnov, E. M. BUSCO: assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness with single-copy orthologs. Bioinformatics 31, 3210â3212 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d58070e1101">38</a></sup>, sequencing of 10 bacterial artificial chromosome (BAC) clones, and comparing to a linkage map (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#MOESM1">5</a>). BUSCO estimates 94.3% completeness (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#MOESM1">3</a>), suggesting that the assembly includes most of the <i>S. tora</i> gene space. BAC sequence alignments showed high mapping rates (99.8%) with the assemblies (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#MOESM1">6</a> and Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#MOESM1">4</a>). We also built a genetic map of diploid <i>S. tora</i>, to which 401.1âMb of the assembled scaffolds were mapped (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#MOESM1">7</a>). The 13 linkage groups matched well to the 13 chromosomes, indicating the high quality of <i>S. tora</i> genome assembly (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#MOESM1">8</a>).</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Summary of genome assembly and protein-coding genes in <i>S. tora</i>.</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p><i>S. tora</i>âs genomic content is consistent with other sequenced plant genomes. A total of 45,268 genes were annotated with the average gene length (3,157 bp), exon sequence length (217 bp with 4.37 exons per gene), and intron length (655 bp) that were similar to those of other legume species (TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#Tab1">1</a> and Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#MOESM1">9</a>). Among the protein-coding genes, 31,010 (68.50%) showed homology to characterized genes based on BLAST searches and 25,453 (56.23%) and 17,450 (38.55%) were assigned to Gene Ontology (GO) terms and KEGG pathways, respectively (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#MOESM1">5</a>). As expected, the genes were unevenly distributed with an increase in density toward the ends of the pseudomolecules (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#Fig1">1a</a>). We also identified genes encoding for 839 tRNA, 752 rRNA, 3,278 lncRNA (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#Fig1">1a</a>, Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#MOESM1">6</a>, and Supplementary DataÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#MOESM5">1</a>), and 1,644 transcription factors (TFs) from 36 families that accounted for 3.63% of the protein-coding genes (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#MOESM1">7</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="The S. tora genome and comparative genomic analysis."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 1: The <i>S</i>. <i>tora</i> genome and comparative genomic analysis.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p><b>a</b> The landscape of genome assembly (~502âMb) and annotation of <i>S. tora</i>. Tracks (from outside) correspond to chromosomes (Chr01âChr13 on a Mb scale), gene density, repeat density, rRNA density, tRNA density, GC content, and GC skew. Tracks are drawn in nonoverlapping 100-kb sliding windows. The red bars in the rRNA and tRNA tracks represent the maximum density of copies on the scale. <b>b</b> An overview of orthologous and paralogous genes among <i>S. tora</i>, related legumes, and <i>V. vinifera</i>. âUncertainâ indicates homologous genes obtained from BLAST but not found using OrthoMCL. âSpecies-specificâ genes do not have any similarity to genes in the other species based on BLAST and OrthoMCL. <b>c</b> Significantly enriched biological process GO and KEGG categories (specialized metabolism) of expanded gene families in <i>S. tora</i>. <b>d</b> Lineage-specific expansion of the CHS-L gene family in <i>S. tora</i> and four other legumes. The 15 tandemly duplicated gene clusters are ordered and shown on chromosome 7, as well as one gene on chromosome 2.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Comparative genomics and gene family evolution analysis</h3><p>To assess candidate gene families involved in anthraquinone biosynthesis, we compared the <i>S. tora</i> genome with those from 15 related plant species. Reciprocal pairwise comparisons<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Altschul, S. F., Gish, W., Miller, W., Myers, E. W. &amp; Lipman, D. J. Basic local alignment search tool. J. Mol. Biol. 215, 403â410 (1990)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d58070e1502">39</a></sup> of the 16 species (15 legumes and grapevine) revealed that <i>S. tora</i> has the most species-specific genes of all the 16 plants compared, with 7,231 (15.9%) genes that are specific to <i>S. tora</i> (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#Fig1">1b</a>, Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#MOESM1">10</a>, and Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#MOESM1">8</a>). We compared gene family expansion and contraction across the species to identify gene families that were expanded or contracted in <i>S. tora</i>. Of the 36,597 gene families found among the sixteen species, 2,874 and 3,371 gene families were expanded and contracted in <i>S. tora</i>, respectively (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#MOESM1">11</a>). The gene families that were expanded in <i>S. tora</i> were enriched for several Gene Ontology (GO) and KEGG terms, including those involved in specialized metabolism including âphenylpropanoid biosynthesis,â âisoflavonoid biosynthesis,â and âterpene biosynthesis,â likely reflecting the importance of genes for the biosynthesis of phenolics, isoflavonoids, and terpenoids in <i>S. tora</i> (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#Fig1">1c</a> and Supplementary DataÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#MOESM6">2</a>). To investigate <i>S. tora</i> metabolism further, we developed a genome-scale metabolic network database of <i>S. tora</i> named StoraCyc and identified enriched metabolic pathways. Expanded gene families in <i>S. tora</i> were enriched in phenolic and nitrogen-containing specialized metabolism, cofactor, carbohydrate, and hormone metabolism of StoraCyc (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#MOESM1">12</a> and Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#MOESM1">9</a>). We also examined enriched metabolic domains in families that are expanded only in <i>S. tora</i>, rapidly expanded in <i>S. tora</i>, and rapidly expanded only in <i>S. tora</i>. Phenolic specialized metabolism was the only domain of metabolism enriched in all these families (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#MOESM1">12</a> and Supplementary DataÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#MOESM7">3</a>).</p><p>We next probed which of the lineage-specifically expanded families might be involved in anthraquinone biosynthesis. In plants, type III polyketide synthases such as chalcone synthases (CHSs) are involved in the biosynthesis of plant specialized metabolites, particularly acetate-pathway-derived flavonoids, stilbenes, and aromatic polyphenols<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Abdel-Rahman, I. A. M. et al. In vitro formation of the anthranoid scaffold by cell-free extracts from yeast-extract-treated Cassia bicapsularis cell cultures. Phytochemistry 88, 15â24 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d58070e1579">33</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Anguraj Vadivel, A. K., Krysiak, K., Tian, G. &amp; Dhaubhadel, S. Genome-wide identification and localization of chalcone synthase family in soybean (Glycine max [L]Merr). BMC Plant Biol. 18, 325â325 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d58070e1582">40</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Pandith, S. A. et al. Functional promiscuity of two divergent paralogs of type III plant polyketide synthases. Plant Physiol. 171, 2599â2619 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d58070e1585">41</a></sup>. The <i>S. tora</i> CHS family contains twelve CHS (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#MOESM1">13</a>) and sixteen CHS-L genes (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#MOESM1">13</a> and Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#MOESM1">10</a>). Interestingly, the CHS-L gene family specifically and rapidly expanded only in the <i>S. tora</i> genome (16 genes in <i>S. tora</i>, 5 in <i>C. fasciculata</i>, 2 in <i>A. hypogaea</i>, 1 in <i>M. truncatula</i>, 1 in <i>G. max</i>, and none in the other 11 species) (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#Fig1">1d</a> and Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#MOESM1">11</a>). Twelve of the CHS-L genes are specific to the <i>S. tora</i> lineage and the majority of <i>S. tora</i> CHS-L genes (15 of 16) are distributed only in chromosome 7 and arranged in tandem (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#Fig1">1d</a>). Interestingly, CHS genes contracted in the <i>S. tora</i> genome (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#MOESM1">11</a>). Even though carminic acid (C-glucosylated anthraquinone) was produced in <i>Nicotiana</i> plantsÂ by combining an octaketide synthase gene from <i>Aloe arborescens</i>, two cyclases from <i>Streptomyces</i>, and a glycosyltransferase from an insect<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Andersen-Ranberg, J. et al. Synthesis of C-glucosylated octaketide anthraquinones in Nicotiana benthamiana by using a multispecies-based biosynthetic pathway. Chembiochem 18, 1893â1897 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d58070e1652">42</a></sup>, direct evidence of anthraquinone biosynthesis using plant CHS enzymes has not been established so far. However, several studies speculated the involvement of CHS-Ls in synthesizing anthraquinones<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Abdel-Rahman, I. A. M. et al. In vitro formation of the anthranoid scaffold by cell-free extracts from yeast-extract-treated Cassia bicapsularis cell cultures. Phytochemistry 88, 15â24 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d58070e1656">33</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Karppinen, K., Hokkanen, J., Mattila, S., Neubauer, P. &amp; Hohtola, A. Octaketide-producing type III polyketide synthase from Hypericum perforatum is expressed in dark glands accumulating hypericins. FEBS J. 275, 4329â4342 (2008)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d58070e1659">34</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Andersen-Ranberg, J. et al. Synthesis of C-glucosylated octaketide anthraquinones in Nicotiana benthamiana by using a multispecies-based biosynthetic pathway. Chembiochem 18, 1893â1897 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d58070e1662">42</a></sup>. At the genomic level, we found that the CHS-L gene family expanded most notably in <i>S. tora</i>, which may explain in part why <i>S. tora</i> is rich in anthraquinones.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Metabolite profiling and transcriptomics of seed development</h3><p>To test the hypothesis that CHS-Ls might be involved in anthraquinone biosynthesis in <i>S. tora</i>, we turned to the tissue that is enriched in anthraquinones, the seed. We profiled anthranoids from seven developmental stages of the seed (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#Fig2">2a</a>), using ten standard anthraquinones (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#MOESM1">12</a>) as references for quantification. Anthraquinone accumulation varied in each stage (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#Fig2">2b</a>). Importantly, the profile shifted toward modified derivatives such as glucoaurantio-obtusin, aurantio-obtusin, obtusifolin, and chryso-obtusin during late stages of seed development (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#Fig2">2b</a> and Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#MOESM1">13</a>) essentially becoming major storage metabolites in dry seeds.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Analysis of anthraquinone contents and CHS-L gene expression during S. tora seed development."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 2: Analysis of anthraquinone contents and CHS-L gene expression during <i>S. tora</i> seed development.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-020-19681-1/MediaObjects/41467_2020_19681_Fig2_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-020-19681-1/MediaObjects/41467_2020_19681_Fig2_HTML.png" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="568" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p><b>a</b> Developmental progression of <i>S. tora</i> seeds (Stage 1âStage 7). <b>b</b> Concentrations of ten anthraquinones during the seven developmental stages of <i>S</i>. <i>tora</i> seeds (meanâÂ±âSD, <i>n</i>â=â3). Dots represent individual values. <b>c</b>, <b>d</b> Scaled transcript expression profiles (in transcripts per million, TPM) of cluster 3 (554 genes) and cluster 6 (1,513 genes) during seed development and enriched metabolic domains within these two clusters. <b>e</b> Expression analysis of CHS-L genes during seed development. Heatmap represents normalized transcripts per million (TPM) from two biological replicates. S1âS7 represents the seed-development stages of <i>S. tora</i>. The source data underlying Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#Fig2">2b</a> are provided as a Source Data file.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>To identify genes involved in the biosynthesis of anthraquinones during seed development, we performed transcriptome and metabolome analysis from developing seeds. The majority (68%) of genes decreased in expression during seed maturation (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#MOESM1">14</a> and Supplementary DataÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#MOESM8">4</a>). Similarly, metabolic gene expression decreased across all metabolic domains during seed maturation (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#MOESM1">15</a>), consistent with metabolite-profiling results, which showed that the majority of primary metabolites involved in central carbon metabolism were reduced after stage 4 (Supplementary Figs.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#MOESM1">16</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#MOESM1">17</a>, and Supplementary DataÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#MOESM9">5</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#MOESM10">6</a>). However, some genes increased in expression during seed maturation (32% genes represented by 5 clusters, Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#MOESM1">14</a> and Supplementary DataÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#MOESM11">7</a>), which we reasoned would be enriched in anthraquinone biosynthetic enzymes. To identify genes that showed similar expression patterns as anthraquinone biosynthesis, we first identified all genes that were differentially expressed relative to stage 1 during seed development. Co-expression analysis of differentially expressed genes during seed development detected nine co-expression clusters (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#MOESM1">14</a>). Among them, clusters 3 and 6 showed similar patterns to anthraquinone accumulation in which genes were highly induced starting stage 5 (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#Fig2">2c, d</a>). Cluster 6 was statistically overrepresented with genes annotated as transferases, UDP-glycosyltransferases, and oxidoreductases, which may reflect enzymes involved in the tailoring of anthraquinones to produce gluco-obtusifolin, glucoaurantio-obtusin, and other derivatives including aurantio-obtusin (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#Fig2">2b</a> and Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#MOESM1">14</a>). In addition, genes in clusters 3 and 6 were enriched with specialized, fatty acid and lipid, cofactor, and carbohydrate metabolism in StoraCyc (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#Fig2">2c, d</a>, and Supplementary DataÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#MOESM11">7</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Identification of a candidate anthraquinone synthase family</h3><p>With these data in hand, we searched specifically for CHS-L genes that were induced in stage 4 when the primary metabolite levels decrease and anthraquinones start to accumulate. Among the 16 CHS-L genes, two genes (STO07G228250 (CHS-L9) and STO07G228220 (CHS-L6)) showed high expression levels at stage 4 (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#Fig2">2e</a> and Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#MOESM1">15</a>), where anthraquinone contents started to accumulate in seeds (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#MOESM1">13</a>). Both of these genes share high amino acid sequence similarities with each other and with STO02G027180, the gene that was highly expressed at stage 6 of seed development (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#Fig2">2e</a>). Further comparison of sequence alignment and phylogenetic tree analysis with previously characterized octaketide synthases, HpPKS and ArOKS, which were presumed to be involved in hypericin and barbaloin biosynthesis based on the production of the octaketide shunt products<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Mizuuchi, Y. et al. Novel type III polyketide synthases from Aloe arborescens. FEBS J. 276, 2391â2401 (2009)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d58070e1827">35</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Pillai, P. P. &amp; Nair, A. R. Hypericin biosynthesis in Hypericum hookerianum Wight and Arn: investigation on biochemical pathways using metabolite inhibitors and suppression subtractive hybridization. C. R. Biol. 337, 571â580 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d58070e1830">37</a></sup>, showed that STO07G228250 (CHS-L9) was more similar to them than the other two CHS-Ls (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#MOESM1">18</a>). Therefore, we hypothesized that STO07G228250 (CHS-L9) could be engaged in anthraquinone biosynthesis in <i>S. tora</i>. As a control, we also selected a member of the CHS family, STO03G058250 (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#MOESM1">18</a>), presumed to be involved in flavonoid biosynthesis, for biochemistry experiments.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Biochemical confirmation of an anthraquinone enzyme class</h3><p>To perform enzymatic assays, we expressed STO07G228250 (CHS-L9) and STO03G058250 (CHS) heterologously in <i>E. coli</i> and purified them to homogeneity (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#MOESM1">19</a>). Enzyme assays were conducted in a phosphate buffer saline containing malonyl-CoA for successive condensation reactions to produce polyketides. STO07G228250 (CHS-L9)-catalyzed reaction mixture revealed the existence of two molecules with a molecular mass of 319.08 Da and 301.07 Da (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#Fig3">3</a>). Neither of these metabolites was detected in reactions containing STO03G058250 (CHS) or heat-denatured STO07G228250 (CHS-L9) (control), indicating that these two masses are most likely the products of the PKS-catalyzed reaction (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#Fig3">3</a>). The ESI-MS spectrum showed a compound with a distinct peak at <i>m/z</i><sup>+</sup> 319.0827 (retention time (<i>t</i><sub>R</sub>) 4.45âmin), which corresponds exactly to the mass of atrochrysone carboxylic acid (C<sub>16</sub>H<sub>14</sub>O<sub>7</sub> with 319.0818âDa in the proton-adduct mode). Furthermore, the theoretical isotope model for the same chemical formula corroborated perfectly to the observed isotope mass (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#MOESM1">20</a>). Likewise, the ESI-MS spectrum of the latter metabolite (<i>t</i><sub>R</sub> 4.62âmin) <i>m/z</i><sup>+</sup> 301.0715 matched to the mass of endocrocin anthrone (C<sub>16</sub>H<sub>12</sub>O<sub>6</sub> with calculated exact mass of 301.0712âDa) for which the theoretical isotope mass model and observed mass isotope were perfectly aligned (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#MOESM1">20</a>). To further verify these metabolites as PKS-derived products, a set of reactions were conducted with STO07G228250 (CHS-L9) and heat-denatured STO07G228250 (dead CHS-L9) containing <sup>13</sup>C<sub>3</sub>-malonyl-CoA as substrate. The EIC for all carbon-labeled atrochrysone carboxylic acid (<sup>13</sup>C<sub>16</sub>H<sub>14</sub>O<sub>7</sub>, exact mass: 335.1355âDa) (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#Fig3">3</a> and Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#MOESM1">20</a>) and endocrocin anthrone (<sup>13</sup>C<sub>16</sub>H<sub>12</sub>O<sub>6</sub>, exact mass: 317.1249âDa) (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#Fig3">3</a> and Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#MOESM1">20</a>) was confirmed to be present in only theÂ CHS-L9 catalyzed reaction. The observed ESI-MS spectra aligned to the theoretical isotope mass of the corresponding metabolites. Except for these two metabolites, none of the other octaketide metabolites, such as emodin anthrone, endocrocin, emodin, chrysophanol, or islandicin, was produced even when NADPH was added in the reaction mixtures.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Enzyme assays and MS analysis of anthraquinones."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 3: Enzyme assays and MS analysis of anthraquinones.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-020-19681-1/MediaObjects/41467_2020_19681_Fig3_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-020-19681-1/MediaObjects/41467_2020_19681_Fig3_HTML.png" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="300" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p><b>a</b> (i) Extracted ion chromatograms (EIC) for the compound with the mass 319.08âDa in reaction mixtures containing malonyl-CoA as substrate and chalcone synthase-like (CHS-L9) (STO07G228250), chalcone synthase (CHS) (STO03G058250), or heat-denatured CHS-L9. (ii) EIC for the mass 335.13 Da in reaction mixtures containing <sup>13</sup>C<sub>3</sub>-malonyl-CoA containing CHS-L9 or heat-denatured CHS-L9 enzyme. Dots in the structure represent <sup>13</sup>C-labeled carbons. <b>b</b> (i) EIC for the mass 301.07 Da in reaction mixtures containing malonyl-CoA as substrate containing CHS-L9, CHS, or heat-denatured CHS-L9. The inset shows a zoomed region of the EIC chromatogram. (ii) EIC for the mass 317.12âDa in reaction mixtures containing <sup>13</sup>C<sub>3</sub>-malonyl-CoA containing CHS-L9 or heat-denatured CHS-L9 enzyme. <b>c</b> PKS-mediated biosynthetic pathway of anthraquinones. Two pathway intermediates, atrochrysone carboxylic acid and endocrocin anthrone, were produced in the CHS-L9-catalyzed reaction mixture.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Previous studies reported the production of derailment products (SEK4 and SEK4b) by plant octaketide synthases such as HpPKS2 and ArOKS (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#MOESM1">1</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Abdel-Rahman, I. A. M. et al. In vitro formation of the anthranoid scaffold by cell-free extracts from yeast-extract-treated Cassia bicapsularis cell cultures. Phytochemistry 88, 15â24 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d58070e1981">33</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Karppinen, K., Hokkanen, J., Mattila, S., Neubauer, P. &amp; Hohtola, A. Octaketide-producing type III polyketide synthase from Hypericum perforatum is expressed in dark glands accumulating hypericins. FEBS J. 275, 4329â4342 (2008)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d58070e1984">34</a></sup>. We performed TOF-ESI-MS<sup>2</sup> analysis for both of the precursor ions 319 and 301 and compared them to mass fragments of emodin and aloe-emodin (Supplementary Figs.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#MOESM1">21</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#MOESM1">22</a>), which share similar anthranoid scaffold and are known to be derived from atrochrysone carboxylic acid and endocrocin anthrone (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#MOESM1">1</a>). The ESI-MS<sup>2</sup> fragments of the precursor ions 319 (Supplementary Figs.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#MOESM1">23</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#MOESM1">24</a>) and 301 (Supplementary Figs.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#MOESM1">25</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#MOESM1">26</a>) shared most of the fragments with the emodin and aloe-emodin. However, TOF-ESI-MS<sup>2</sup> sister fragments of SEK4 and SEK4b reported previously<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Karppinen, K., Hokkanen, J., Mattila, S., Neubauer, P. &amp; Hohtola, A. Octaketide-producing type III polyketide synthase from Hypericum perforatum is expressed in dark glands accumulating hypericins. FEBS J. 275, 4329â4342 (2008)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d58070e2017">34</a></sup> did not align to any of the fragments of standard anthraquinones, nor to atrochrysone carboxylic acid and endocrocin anthrone produced in the reactions. These evidences indicate that the metabolites produced in the reaction mixture are anthranoid scaffolds, not the octaketide shunt products. Altogether, these results indicate that STO07G228250 (CHS-L9), a type III PKS, carries out the first committed step of anthraquinone biosynthesis via polyketide pathway in plants. We could not detect a final product such as emodin or other fully oxidized products in the reaction mixture. The complete biosynthesis might need additional enzymes for decarboxylation and oxidation. It is possible that other CHS-L genes might also participate in anthraquinone biosynthesis. Unlike in plants, anthraquinones are produced via type II PKS enzymes in bacteria such as <i>Streptomyces</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Hu, Y., Martinez, E. D. &amp; MacMillan, J. B. Anthraquinones from a marine-derived Streptomyces spinoverrucosus. J. Nat. Products 75, 1759â1764 (2012)." href="#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d58070e2023">43</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Yan, X. et al. Cloning and heterologous expression of three type II PKS gene clusters from Streptomyces bottropensis. Chembiochem 13, 224â230 (2012)." href="#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d58070e2023_1">44</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Zhang, C. et al. Biosynthetic BaeyerâVilliger chemistry enables access to two anthracene scaffolds from a single gene cluster in deep-sea-derived Streptomyces olivaceus SCSIO T05. J. Nat. Products 81, 1570â1577 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d58070e2026">45</a></sup>, <i>Photorhabdus luminescens</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Brachmann, A. O. et al. A type II polyketide synthase is responsible for anthraquinone biosynthesis in Photorhabdus luminescens. Chembiochem 8, 1721â1728 (2007)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d58070e2032">46</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Zhou, Q. et al. Molecular mechanism of polyketide shortening in anthraquinone biosynthesis of Photorhabdus luminescens. Chem. Sci. 10, 6341â6349 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d58070e2035">47</a></sup>, and <i>Verrucosispora</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Sottorff, I. et al. Antitumor anthraquinones from an Easter Island Sea Anemone: animal or bacterial origin? Mar. Drugs 17, 154 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d58070e2042">48</a></sup>. Thus, the biosynthetic route of anthraquinones is distinct in plants and other anthraquinone-producing organisms, illustrating a convergent metabolic evolution.</p><p>In summary, the reference genome of <i>S. tora</i> revealed the rapid evolution of putative polyketide synthase genes. By combining metabolomics, transcriptomics, and biochemical characterization of a candidate polyketide synthase, we discovered the anthranoid-forming enzyme in plants. With these tools in hand, elucidation of genes involved in the rest of the anthraquinone biosynthesis pathway in <i>S. tora</i> and other species will be accelerated. These resources can also be used as a platform to develop a medicinally useful cultivar of <i>S. tora</i> with a high content of bioactive molecules.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec8" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec8-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec8">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec8-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec9">DNA sequencing</h3><p>We sequenced a cultivated diploid <i>Senna tora</i> cv. Myeongyun (voucher number: IT89788) grown in Jeonju, Korea (N: 35Â° 49â²; E: 127Â° 09â²). The total DNA was extracted from young fresh leaves of <i>S</i>. <i>tora</i> cv. Myeongyun using the modified cetyltrimethylammonium bromide (CTAB) method<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Allen, G. C., Flores-Vergara, M. A., Krasynanski, S., Kumar, S. &amp; Thompson, W. F. A modified protocol for rapid DNA isolation from plant tissues using cetyltrimethylammonium bromide. Nat. Protoc. 1, 2320â2325 (2006)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d58070e2080">49</a></sup>. DNA purity and concentration were checked by electrophoresis analysis on 1.2% agarose gel and by DropSense96 Spectrophotometer (Trinean, Belgium). A total of 34 single-molecule real-time (SMRT) cells were run on the PacBio RS II system and 5 cells on the Sequel system using P6/C4 chemistry. We generated a total of 80.01âGb of clean reads (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#MOESM1">1</a>).</p><p>Illumina sequencing libraries were prepared according to the Illumina protocols. Briefly, 1âÎ¼g of genomic DNA was fragmented by Covaris. The fragmented DNA was repaired, and the base adenine was ligated to the 3â² end. Illumina adapters were then ligated to the fragments, and the proper samples were selected. The size-selected product was PCR-amplified, and the final product was validated using the Agilent Bioanalyzer. Then we sequenced 200-bp paired-end (PE) and 3â20- kb mate-pair (MP) libraries and 500-bp PE using the HiSeqâ¢ 2500 and MiSeq platforms (Illumina, San Diego, USA), respectively. Finally, we generated a total of 577.93âGb of clean reads for the 200- and 500-bp PE and 3-, 5-, 10-, and 20-kb MP libraries (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#MOESM1">1</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec10">Genome-size estimation</h3><p>Total Illumina DNA sequences were subjected to preprocessing steps, which included adapter trimming, quality trimming (Q20), and contamination removal. Adapter and quality trimming were conducted using Trimmomatic v0.36Â (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Bolger, A. M., Lohse, M. &amp; Usadel, B. Trimmomatic: a flexible trimmer for Illumina sequence data. Bioinformatics 30, 2114â2120 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d58070e2101">50</a></sup>), and <i>S. tora</i> organellar genome contamination of each sample was removed by CLCMapper v4.2.0 (<a href="https://www.qiagenbioinformatics.com/products/clc-assembly-cell/">https://www.qiagenbioinformatics.com/products/clc-assembly-cell/</a>) using the chloroplast genome (Genbank ID: NC_030193) and mitochondria genome sequencesÂ (Genbank ID: NC_038053)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Kang, S.-H., Won, S. Y. &amp; Kim, C.-K. The complete mitochondrial genome sequences of Senna tora (Fabales: Fabaceae). Mitochondrial DNA Part B 4, 1283â1284 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d58070e2115">51</a></sup>. All preprocessed sequences were subjected to genome-size estimation using the <i>k</i>-mer-based method<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Shin, G.-H. et al. First draft genome for Red Sea bream of family Sparidae. Front. Genet. 9, 643 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d58070e2123">52</a></sup>. The <i>k</i>-mer frequencies (<i>k</i>-mer sizeâ=â21) obtained using the Jellyfish v2.0 method<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="MarÃ§ais, G. &amp; Kingsford, C. A fast, lock-free approach for efficient parallel counting of occurrences of k-mers. Bioinformatics 27, 764â770 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d58070e2133">53</a></sup>, and the genome size was calculated by using the following formulas: (1) genome-coverage depthâ=â(<i>k</i>-mer coverage depthâÃâaverage read length)/(average read lengthâââ<i>k</i>-mer sizeâ+â1), and (2) genome sizeâ=âtotal base number/genome-coverage depth. A total of 27.5âGb of clean Illumina reads from the 200-bp PE library were used to determine the genome size of <i>S. tora</i>. In this study, the distribution of 21 <i>k</i>-mer showed a major peak at 50Ã. According to the total number of <i>k</i>-mers and the corresponding <i>k</i>-mer depth, the <i>S. tora</i> genome size was estimated to be ~547.02âMb (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#MOESM1">2</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec11">Genome assembly</h3><p>High-quality PE and MP sequences (Phred score &gt;20) were obtained by removing low-quality sequences and duplicated reads from whole-genome NGS data. Three de novo assemblers, SOAPdenovo v2.04Â (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Luo, R. et al. SOAPdenovo2: an empirically improved memory-efficient short-read de novo assembler. Gigascience 1, 18 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d58070e2170">54</a></sup>), Allpaths-LG v48777Â (refs. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Butler, J. et al. ALLPATHS: de novo assembly of whole-genome shotgun microreads. Genome Res. 18, 810â820 (2008)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d58070e2174">55</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="MacCallum, I. et al. ALLPATHS 2: small genomes assembled accurately and with high continuity from short paired reads. Genome Biol. 10, R103 (2009)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d58070e2177">56</a></sup>), and Platanus v1.2.1Â (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Kajitani, R. et al. Efficient de novo assembly of highly heterozygous genomes from whole-genome shotgun short reads. Genome Res. 24, 1384â1395 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d58070e2181">57</a></sup>), were performed using default parameters. For scaffolding of contig sequences, mate-pair (MP) reads were mapped to contig sequences and scaffold sequences were generated using SSPACE v3.0 with default parameters. To validate scaffold sequences, MP reads were remapped to the scaffold sequences and misscaffold sequences were disassembled into initial contig sequences using an in-house script.</p><p>The average coverage of SMRT sequences was about 146Ã by using RS II and Sequel systems. An average subread length was about 9 kb and the maximum length was 104.5âkb. We removed the sequences of <i>S. tora</i> organellar genomes. Then, the filtered subread sequences were assembled de novo using the diploid assembly FALCON v0.4 assembler<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Chin, C.-S. et al. Phased diploid genome assembly with single-molecule real-time sequencing. Nat. Methods 13, 1050â1054 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d58070e2191">58</a></sup>. To increase the assembly accuracy, the length cut-off option was specified based on the subreadsâ N50 value of 14 kb and contigs were further corrected with Sequel data by Arrow (<a href="https://github.com/PacificBiosciences/GenomicConsensus">https://github.com/PacificBiosciences/GenomicConsensus</a>, v2.1.0). To improve the quality of genome assembly results, we also performed error correction using default parameters of BWA and GATKâs FastaAlternativeReferenceMaker<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="DePristo, M. A. et al. A framework for variation discovery and genotyping using next-generation DNA sequencing data. Nat. Genet. 43, 491â498 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d58070e2202">59</a></sup> with haplotig-merged primary contigs and 101.2Ã Illumina reads (PE_200bp).</p><p>To obtain the best possible draft sequence, we compared the results obtained by SOAPdenovo2, Allpaths-LG, Platanus, and FALCON algorithms. De novo assembly by Platanus and FALCON outperformed the results produced by SOAPdenovo2 and Allpaths-LG (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#MOESM1">2</a>). The number of contigs was lower, N50 length was longer, the assembled size was close to the estimated genome size, and their contiguity statistics were higher. With the assembly obtained by Platanus and FALCON, we also assessed the quality of genome assembly using Benchmarking Universal Single Copy Orthologs (BUSCO)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="SimÃ£o, F. A., Waterhouse, R. M., Ioannidis, P., Kriventseva, E. V. &amp; Zdobnov, E. M. BUSCO: assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness with single-copy orthologs. Bioinformatics 31, 3210â3212 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d58070e2212">38</a></sup>. The percentage of complete proteins was 90.6% for the Platanus assembly and 94.3% for the FALCON assembly (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#MOESM1">3</a>). Based on these criteria, the assembly developed using FALCON assembler was chosen for the genome annotation.
