We evaluated the quality of the assembly by mapping the Illumina reads back to the scaffolds (99.7%) and expressed sequence tag (EST) sequences mapping to the scaffolds (97.2% of Iso-Seq and 89.9% of RNA-Seq) (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#MOESM1">16</a>), supporting the high quality of the <i>S. tora</i> genome assembly.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec12">Physical map validation with BAC libraries</h3><p>To validate the assembled genome against a physical map, we generated bacterial artificial chromosome (BAC) libraries. First, 15âg of young fresh leaves were harvested from growth-room-grown <i>S. tora</i> cv. Myeongyoun plants that have been placed in the dark for 48âh to reduce carbohydrate concentration, which may cause carryover contamination and be detrimental to subsequent enzyme reactions. Fresh leaf tissues were ground to a fine powder in liquid nitrogen using a pestle and mortar. Leaf tissues were transferred immediately to an ice-cold lysis buffer and gently stirred to extract nuclei. The nuclei were embedded in agarose plugs and transferred to proteinase K buffer to obtain high-molecular-weight (HMW) DNA. The HMW DNA was partially digested using <i>Hin</i>dIII- and <i>Bam</i>HI-restriction enzymes and underwent a size selection three times in order to obtain consistently large inserts. Size-selected DNA was ligated with pSMART BAC vector and transformed into DH10B-competent cells. The <i>Hin</i>dIII BAC library has an average insert size of 95 kb and a titer of 1.6âÃâ10<sup>6</sup>. Certified BAC clones were colonized on agar medium and cultured in liquid medium supplemented with chloramphenicol.</p><p>The ten BAC clones were completely sequenced using 454 Life Sciences GS FLX System (GS FLX) and ABI 3730Ãl DNA Analyzer. Analyzed sequencing data were assembled using Newbler v2.8 (<a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/assembly/GCA_000507345.1/">https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/assembly/GCA_000507345.1/</a>) and used to create contigs or scaffolds. To fill the gap of sequences, we used primer walking<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Kaczorowski, T. &amp; Szybalski, W. Genomic DNA sequencing by SPEL-6 primer walking using hexamer ligation. Gene 223, 83â91 (1998)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d58070e2257">60</a></sup>. The primer walking method has been widely used in genome sequencingÂ projects to determine the order of contigs and connect the remaining sequence gaps between the contigs. This way, a draft sequence for ten BAC clones was created. Finally, completed BAC clone sequences were checked by using the HiSeq sequence data for sequence error correction. To validate the genome assembly obtained by FALCON, we performed anÂ all-by-all alignment (-minIdentityâ=â80â99, -minScoreâ=â100, -fastMap) of the 10 complete BACs and the assemblies using BLAT v3.2.4Â (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Kent, W. J. BLATâthe BLAST-like alignment tool. Genome Res. 12, 656â664 (2002)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d58070e2261">61</a></sup>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13">Genotype-by-sequencing linkage analysis</h3><p>Genomic DNA was extracted from the two parents (<i>S. tora</i> cv. Myeongyun (voucher number: IT89788) and ST-9 (voucher number: IT104602)) and 153 F2 progeny using a Qiagen plant DNAeasy kit. Two genotype-by-sequencing (GBS) libraries were prepared using <i>Ape</i>KI restriction enzyme as described in Elshire et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Elshire, R. J. et al. A robust, simple genotyping-by-sequencing (GBS) approach for high diversity species. PLoS ONE 6, e19379 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d58070e2280">62</a></sup>. The GBS libraries (74 F2 individualsâ+âtwo parents; 79 F2 individualsâ+âtwo parents) were sequenced on an Illumina HiSeq2500 system. Low-quality bases and adapter sequences were trimmed using Trimmomatic v0.36Â (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Bolger, A. M., Lohse, M. &amp; Usadel, B. Trimmomatic: a flexible trimmer for Illumina sequence data. Bioinformatics 30, 2114â2120 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d58070e2284">50</a></sup>) and the trimmed reads from each sample were mapped to the <i>S. tora</i> draft assembly using BWA-MEM<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Li, H. &amp; Durbin, R. Fast and accurate long-read alignment with Burrows-Wheeler transform. Bioinformatics 26, 589â595 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d58070e2292">63</a></sup>. HaplotypeCaller in GATK<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="DePristo, M. A. et al. A framework for variation discovery and genotyping using next-generation DNA sequencing data. Nat. Genet. 43, 491â498 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d58070e2296">59</a></sup> was used to call single-nucleotide polymorphisms (SNPs) and generate a raw vcf file. High-quality biallelic SNPs were selected using VCFtools<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="Danecek, P. et al. The variant call format and VCFtools. Bioinformatics 27, 2156â2158 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d58070e2300">64</a></sup> with the following conditions: (1) minimum read depth â¥ 5, (2) minimum genotype quality â¥ 20, and (3) missing genotype â¤ 30%. The SNP positions that showed polymorphic homozygous SNPs between the parents were retained for linkage analysis. Linkage analysis was conducted using QTL IciMapping v4.1Â (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Meng, L., Li, H., Zhang, L. &amp; Wang, J. QTL IciMapping: integrated software for genetic linkage map construction and quantitative trait locus mapping in biparental populations. Crop J. 3, 269â283 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d58070e2304">65</a></sup>) with the Kosambi function.</p><p>A total of 721.8 million raw PE reads were generated from two <i>Apek</i>I GBS libraries, and 372 million trimmed PE reads were used for subsequent linkage analysis. Of those, about 89.8% reads were mapped to the <i>S. tora</i> reference assembly and 88.6% (329.5 million reads) were concordantly mapped, which was representing about 2.1 million properly mapped PE reads per sample. The GATK HaplotypeCaller called 289,768 and 4.78 million unfiltered variants from libraries 1 and 2, respectively. After low-quality SNPs were filtered, 7,584 and 15,604 high-quality SNPs were obtained from libraries 1 and 2, respectively, and 5,071 markers were commonly represented in both. Three genetics maps independently constructed with three sets of SNP markers (from libraries 1 and 2 and common) were evaluated in terms of a number of anchored contigs and genome representation, and the map that used common markers between libraries 1 and 2 was selected for further analysis. This map contained 2,654 nonredundant markers representing 3,587âcM within 12 linkage groups (LG13 contained only one marker). With this linkage map, we tried to regroup markers by increasing group number parameters from 13 to 25; however, the efforts were not successful to make the 13th linkage group. Finally, the linkage map was compared to pseudochromosomes constructed by Hi-C and we were able to split LG5 into two groups: one with three contigs (164 markers covering 34.4âMb) and the other with 8 contigs (263 markers covering about 44âMb). The final <i>S. tora</i> genetic map with Hi-C information resulted in 13 linkage groups with 4,455 markers spanning 2,780âcM of genetic distance (Supplementary DataÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#MOESM12">8</a>). It enabled to anchor 111 contigs (contigÂ 3 split into two contigs: c31-1 and c31-2) to 13 linkage groups, which represented about 401âMb of <i>S. tora</i> sequence assembly (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#MOESM1">17</a>). Genetically, LG8 was the longest linkage group (347.5âcM) followed by LG13 (343.8âcM) and LG5 (313âcM). Whereas, 487 markers anchored about 45âMb of sequences in LG5, which was the longest anchored chromosome. Physical distance per genetic distance was calculated as 144âkb/cM on average across the whole genome (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#MOESM1">7</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec14">Hi-C technology-assisted pseudochromosome construction</h3><p>To generate pseudochromosomes, chromatin-conformation capture (Hi-C) data were generated using a Phase Genomics (Seattle, WA) Proximo Hi-C Plant Kit, which is a commercially available version of the Hi-C protocol<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="Lieberman-Aiden, E. et al. Comprehensive mapping of long-range interactions reveals folding principles of the human genome. Science 326, 289â293 (2009)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d58070e2341">66</a></sup>. Intact cells from two samples were cross-linked using a formaldehyde solution, digested using the <i>Sau</i>3AI restriction enzyme, and proximity-ligated with biotinylated nucleotides to create chimeric molecules composed of fragments from different regions of the genome that were physically proximal in vivo, but not necessarily genomically proximal. Continuing with the manufacturerâs protocol, molecules were pulled down with streptavidin beads and processed into an Illumina-compatible sequencing library. Sequencing was performed on an Illumina NextSeq 500 (Illumina, San Diego, USA), generating a total of 188,501,285 PE read pairs.</p><p>Reads were aligned to the reference assembly following the manufacturerâs recommendations (<a href="https://phasegenomics.hithub.io/2019/09/19/hic-alignment-and-qc.html">https://phasegenomics.hithub.io/2019/09/19/hic-alignment-and-qc.html</a>). Briefly, reads were aligned using BWA-MEM with the â5SP and -t 8 options specified, and all other options as default. SAMBLASTER<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 67" title="Faust, G. G. &amp; Hall, I. M. SAMBLASTER: fast duplicate marking and structural variant read extraction. Bioinformatics 30, 2503â2505 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#ref-CR67" id="ref-link-section-d58070e2358">67</a></sup> was used to flag PCR duplicates, which were later excluded from further analysis. Alignments were then filtered with samtools<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 68" title="Li, H. et al. The sequence alignment/map format and SAMtools. Bioinformatics 25, 2078â2079 (2009)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#ref-CR68" id="ref-link-section-d58070e2362">68</a></sup> using the -F 2304 filtering flag to remove non-primary and secondary alignments.</p><p>Phase Genomicsâ Proximo Hi-C genome scaffolding platform was used to create chromosome-scale scaffolds from the corrected assembly<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 69" title="Bickhart, D. M. et al. Single-molecule sequencing and chromatin conformation capture enable de novo reference assembly of the domestic goat genome. Nat. Genet 49, 643â650 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#ref-CR69" id="ref-link-section-d58070e2369">69</a></sup>. Similar to the LACHESIS method<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 70" title="Burton, J. N. et al. Chromosome-scale scaffolding of de novo genome assemblies based on chromatin interactions. Nat. Biotechnol. 31, 1119â1125 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#ref-CR70" id="ref-link-section-d58070e2373">70</a></sup>, this process computes a contact frequency matrix from the aligned Hi-C read pairs, normalized by the number of <i>Sau</i>3AI restriction sites (GATC) on each contig, and constructs scaffolds in such a way as to optimize the expected contact frequency and other statistical patterns in Hi-C data. Approximately 20,000 separate Proximo runs were performed to optimize the number of scaffolds and scaffold construction in order to make the scaffolds as concordant with the observed Hi-C data as possible. The Hi-C sequences were aligned to the draft contig assemblies. Finally, Juicebox<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 71" title="Durand, N. C. et al. Juicebox provides a visualization system for Hi-C contact maps with unlimited zoom. Cell Syst. 3, 99â101 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#ref-CR71" id="ref-link-section-d58070e2380">71</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 72" title="Rao, S. S. P. et al. A 3D map of the human genome at kilobase resolution reveals principles of chromatin looping. Cell 159, 1665â1680 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#ref-CR72" id="ref-link-section-d58070e2383">72</a></sup> was used to correct scaffolding errors as well as to introduce two new breaks into two putative misjoined contigs (contigs 3 and 110). All contig sequences not anchored to chromosomes were constructed with 100 Nâs as a linker following the order of contig sizes designated chromosome 00 (Chr 00). The length and number of contigs for each chromosome are shown in Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#MOESM1">18</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec15">Repetitive-element analysis</h3><p>Initially, repeat regions were predicted using the de novo method and classified into repeat subclasses. De novo repeat prediction for <i>S. tora</i> was conducted using RepeatModeler (<a href="http://www.repeatmasker.org/RepeatModeler/">http://www.repeatmasker.org/RepeatModeler/</a>), which includes other methods such as RECON<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 73" title="Bao, Z. &amp; Eddy, S. R. Automated de novo identification of repeat sequence families in sequenced genomes. Genome Res. 12, 1269â1276 (2002)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#ref-CR73" id="ref-link-section-d58070e2408">73</a></sup>, RepeatScout<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 74" title="Price, A. L., Jones, N. C. &amp; Pevzner, P. A. De novo identification of repeat families in large genomes. Bioinformatics 21, i351âi358 (2005)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#ref-CR74" id="ref-link-section-d58070e2412">74</a></sup>, and TRF<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 75" title="Benson, G. Tandem repeats finder: a program to analyze DNA sequences. Nucleic Acids Res. 27, 573â580 (1999)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#ref-CR75" id="ref-link-section-d58070e2416">75</a></sup>. Furthermore, the repeats were masked using RepeatMasker v4.0.5 (<a href="http://www.repeatmasker.org/">http://www.repeatmasker.org/</a>) with RMBlastn v2.2.27+ and classified into its subclasses with the reference of Repbase<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 76" title="Bao, W., Kojima, K. K. &amp; Kohany, O. Repbase update, a database of repetitive elements in eukaryotic genomes. Mob. DNA 6, 11â11 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#ref-CR76" id="ref-link-section-d58070e2428">76</a></sup> v20.08 databases (<a href="https://www.girinst.org/repbase/">https://www.girinst.org/repbase/</a>).</p><p>Transposable elements are major components of plant genomes, but they have not been examined in <i>S. tora</i>. The <i>S. tora</i> genome masked 53.9% of the assembly as repeat sequences. Long terminal repeat (LTR) retrotransposons, mainly Gypsy-type LTRs, are the most abundant, occupying 15.6% of the genome (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#MOESM1">19</a>). The fraction of repeat sequences in the genome is very similar to other Leguminosae family plants such as pigeon pea (51.6%)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 77" title="Varshney, R. K. et al. Draft genome sequence of pigeonpea (Cajanus cajan), an orphan legume crop of resource-poor farmers. Nat. Biotechnol. 30, 83â89 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#ref-CR77" id="ref-link-section-d58070e2451">77</a></sup>, mung bean (50.1%)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 78" title="Kang, Y. J. et al. Genome sequence of mungbean and insights into evolution within Vigna species. Nat. Commun. 5, 5443 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#ref-CR78" id="ref-link-section-d58070e2455">78</a></sup>, and chickpea (49.4%)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 79" title="Varshney, R. K. et al. Draft genome sequence of chickpea (Cicer arietinum) provides a resource for trait improvement. Nat. Biotechnol. 31, 240â246 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#ref-CR79" id="ref-link-section-d58070e2460">79</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec16">Genome annotation</h3><p>The genes from the <i>S. tora</i> reference genome were predicted using an in-house gene prediction pipeline, which includes three modules: evidence-based gene modeler, ab initio gene modeler, and consensus gene modeler. To improve the accuracy of gene prediction, we downloaded a total of 118,390 Iso-Seq reads in GenBank SRA database (SRP159435)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Kang, S.-H. et al. De novo transcriptome sequence of Senna tora provides insights into anthraquinone biosynthesis. PLoS ONE 15, e0225564 (2020)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d58070e2475">15</a></sup>. RNA-Seq from five tissues (leaf, root, stem, flower, and dry seed) and Iso-Seq data were aligned against the <i>S. tora</i> genome. Initially, the sequenced transcriptomes were mapped to the <i>S. tora</i> repeat-masked reference genome using Tophat<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 80" title="Trapnell, C. et al. Differential gene and transcript expression analysis of RNA-seq experiments with TopHat and Cufflinks. Nat. Protoc. 7, 562â578 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#ref-CR80" id="ref-link-section-d58070e2485">80</a></sup>, and transcripts/gene structural boundaries were predicted using Cufflink<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 80" title="Trapnell, C. et al. Differential gene and transcript expression analysis of RNA-seq experiments with TopHat and Cufflinks. Nat. Protoc. 7, 562â578 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#ref-CR80" id="ref-link-section-d58070e2490">80</a></sup> and PASA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 81" title="Haas, B. J. et al. Improving the Arabidopsis genome annotation using maximal transcript alignment assemblies. Nucleic Acids Res. 31, 5654â5666 (2003)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#ref-CR81" id="ref-link-section-d58070e2494">81</a></sup>. To train the ab initio gene modeler AUGUSTUS<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 82" title="Stanke, M., SchÃ¶ffmann, O., Morgenstern, B. &amp; Waack, S. Gene prediction in eukaryotes with a generalized hidden Markov model that uses hints from external sources. BMC Bioinformatics 7, 62 (2006)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#ref-CR82" id="ref-link-section-d58070e2498">82</a></sup> and evidence-based gene modeler GENEID<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 83" title="Blanco, E., Parra, G. &amp; GuigÃ³, R. Using geneid to identify genes. Curr. Protoc. BioinformaticsÂ 4, 4.3 (2007)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#ref-CR83" id="ref-link-section-d58070e2502">83</a></sup>, we selected a few genomes using Exonerate<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 84" title="Slater, G. S. C. &amp; Birney, E. Automated generation of heuristics for biological sequence comparison. BMC BioinformaticsÂ 6, 31 (2005)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#ref-CR84" id="ref-link-section-d58070e2506">84</a></sup>. Genomes we used are <i>Abrus precatorius, Arachis hypogaea, Arachis duranensis, Arachis ipaensis, Cajanus cajan, Cicer arietinum, Faidherbia albida, Glycine max, Glycine soja, Lablab purpureus, Lupinus angustifolius, Medicago truncatula, Mucuna pruriens, Phaseolus vulgaris, Prosopis alba, Sclerocarya birrea, Trifolium medium, Trifolium subterraneum, Vigna angularis, Vigna radiata, Vigna subterranea, Vigna unguiculata,</i> and <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i>. Finally, the predicted ab initio gene models, transcript models, and evidence-based gene models were subjected to build consensus gene models. The consensus genes were subjected to functional annotations from biological databases (NCBI-NR databases, SwissProt, Gene Ontology, and KEGG pathways) by using the Blast2GO<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 85" title="GÃ¶tz, S. et al. High-throughput functional annotation and data mining with the Blast2GO suite. Nucleic Acids Res. 36, 3420â3435 (2008)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#ref-CR85" id="ref-link-section-d58070e2517">85</a></sup>. The transcription factor genes were predicted through searching DNA-binding domains using InterProScan v5.36â75.0Â (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 86" title="Jones, P. et al. InterProScan 5: genome-scale protein function classification. Bioinformatics 30, 1236â1240 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#ref-CR86" id="ref-link-section-d58070e2521">86</a></sup>) and the family name assigned through the rules given in PlantTFDB (v5.0, <a href="http://planttfdb.cbi.pku.edu.cn/">http://planttfdb.cbi.pku.edu.cn/</a>). The gene models were supported by 97.2% Iso-Seq data, which comprised 118,390 high-quality isoforms derived from leaf, root, and two different developmental stages of seeds, and 89.9% RNA-Seq data derived from seed, leaf, root, stem, flower, and seven different stages of seeds, suggesting that the assembly includes most of the <i>S. tora</i> gene space (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#MOESM1">20</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec17">Identification of lncRNA</h3><p>A pipeline for lncRNA identification was designed according to a previous study<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 87" title="Jayakodi, M. et al. Genome-wide characterization of long intergenic non-coding RNAs (lincRNAs) provides new insight into viral diseases in honey bees Apis cerana and Apis mellifera. BMC Genomics 16, 680 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#ref-CR87" id="ref-link-section-d58070e2546">87</a></sup>. In brief, among the total transcripts obtained from reference-guided assembly of transcriptome data, transcripts with open-reading frame (ORF) for â¥100 amino acids andÂ â¤200 nucleotides were removed. We also removed sequences with homology to protein sequences based on BLAST search against the SwissProt<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 88" title="Consortium, T. U. UniProt: a worldwide hub of protein knowledge. Nucleic Acids Res. 47, D506âD515 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#ref-CR88" id="ref-link-section-d58070e2550">88</a></sup> and Pfam<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 89" title="El-Gebali, S. et al. The Pfam protein families database in 2019. Nucleic Acids Res. 47, D427âD432 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#ref-CR89" id="ref-link-section-d58070e2554">89</a></sup> protein databases. The coding potential of the remaining sequences was calculated using Coding Potential Calculator (CPC)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 90" title="Kong, L. et al. CPC: assess the protein-coding potential of transcripts using sequence features and support vector machine. Nucleic Acids Res. 35, W345âW349 (2007)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#ref-CR90" id="ref-link-section-d58070e2558">90</a></sup> and transcripts with CPC score â¥ââ1.0 were removed as CPC scores between â1 and 1 are âweak noncodingâ or âweak codingâ. From the remaining transcripts, housekeeping RNAs (tRNA, rRNA, snRNA, and snoRNA) were removed by comparing with RNACentral database<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 91" title="The RNAcentral Consortium. RNAcentral: a hub of information for non-coding RNA sequences. Nucleic Acids Res. 47, D1250âD1251 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#ref-CR91" id="ref-link-section-d58070e2562">91</a></sup> sequences (cutoff <i>E</i> value of 1eâ10), and those completely matching with <i>S. tora</i> reference protein-coding gene sequences were also removed. Finally, we only retained the longest isoform for each gene to obtain the final set of 3,278 lncRNAs (Supplementary DataÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#MOESM5">1</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec18">Phylogenetic tree construction and evolution-rate estimation</h3><p>To understand the evolutionary patterns of the <i>S. tora</i> genome and gene families, we performed comparative genome analysis. We used 14 legume species and one outlier (<i>Vitis vinifera</i>). The OrthoMCL v2.0.9Â (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 92" title="Li, L., Stoeckert, C. J. Jr. &amp; Roos, D. S. OrthoMCL: identification of ortholog groups for eukaryotic genomes. Genome Res. 13, 2178â2189 (2003)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#ref-CR92" id="ref-link-section-d58070e2590">92</a></sup>) method was used to find orthologous groups in the given genomes. The orthologous clusters were obtained byÂ the Markov graph clustering (MCL v14â137) algorithm, through all-vs-all sequence similarity search by BLASTP v2.2.29+ with an <i>E</i>-value cutoff of 1eâ3. The orthologous clusters that contain proteins from all 16 species were subjected to multiple-sequence alignment with MAFFT v7.305b<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 93" title="Katoh, K. &amp; Standley, D. M. MAFFT multiple sequence alignment software version 7: improvements in performance and usability. Mol. Biol. Evol. 30, 772â780 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#ref-CR93" id="ref-link-section-d58070e2597">93</a></sup> and the alignments were corrected with Gblocks v0.91b<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 94" title="Castresana, J. Selection of conserved blocks from multiple alignments for their use in phylogenetic analysis. Mol. Biol. Evol. 17, 540â552 (2000)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#ref-CR94" id="ref-link-section-d58070e2602">94</a></sup>. The phylogenetic tree was reconstructed using IQ-Tree v1.5.0-beta<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 95" title="Nguyen, L.-T., Schmidt, H. A., von Haeseler, A. &amp; Minh, B. Q. IQ-TREE: a fast and effective stochastic algorithm for estimating maximum-likelihood phylogenies. Mol. Biol. Evol. 32, 268â274 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#ref-CR95" id="ref-link-section-d58070e2606">95</a></sup>, using a maximum likelihood method with 1,000 bootstrap iterations. Here, the longest protein in each genome was selected among the proteins in each orthologous cluster. From the trees, the gene pattern changes such as contraction and expansion were observed among the genomes using CAFE v3.1 method<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 96" title="Han, M. V., Thomas, G. W. C., Lugo-Martinez, J. &amp; Hahn, M. W. Estimating gene gain and loss rates in the presence of error in genome assembly and annotation using CAFE 3.Â Mol. Biol. Evol. 30, 1987â1997 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#ref-CR96" id="ref-link-section-d58070e2610">96</a></sup>. Rapid expansion/contraction is indicated by statistically significant and non-random expansion/contraction at <i>P</i>â&lt;â0.01, as described in CAFE<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 96" title="Han, M. V., Thomas, G. W. C., Lugo-Martinez, J. &amp; Hahn, M. W. Estimating gene gain and loss rates in the presence of error in genome assembly and annotation using CAFE 3.Â Mol. Biol. Evol. 30, 1987â1997 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#ref-CR96" id="ref-link-section-d58070e2617">96</a></sup>. The evolutionary divergence timescale of the species was obtained from the clock and Yule model with theÂ JTT substitution model (the gamma category count set to 4), which was implemented in BEAST2 method<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 97" title="Bouckaert, R. et al. BEAST 2.5: An advanced software platform for Bayesian evolutionary analysis.Â PLoS Comput. Biol. 15, e1006650 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#ref-CR97" id="ref-link-section-d58070e2621">97</a></sup>. The calibration priors were set as 58â70 MYA for the common ancestor of <i>S. tora, C. fasciculata, M. pudica, and M. truncatula</i> and 105â115 MYA for the root according to the TimeTree database (<a href="http://timetree.org">http://timetree.org</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec19">Ks analysis</h3><p>To calculate the synonymous-substitution Ks values, we selected the orthologous gene pairs between species and the paralogous pairs within a species from the orthology analysis. The selected proteins were further subjected to multiple-sequence alignment with MAFFT v7.305b<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 93" title="Katoh, K. &amp; Standley, D. M. MAFFT multiple sequence alignment software version 7: improvements in performance and usability. Mol. Biol. Evol. 30, 772â780 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#ref-CR93" id="ref-link-section-d58070e2645">93</a></sup> and corrected with Gblocks v0.91b<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 94" title="Castresana, J. Selection of conserved blocks from multiple alignments for their use in phylogenetic analysis. Mol. Biol. Evol. 17, 540â552 (2000)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#ref-CR94" id="ref-link-section-d58070e2649">94</a></sup>. The corresponding genomic regions of conserved proteins, which were observed from the corrected multiple alignments, were subjected to Ks calculation using ParaAT v2.0Â (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 98" title="Zhang, Z. et al. ParaAT: a parallel tool for constructing multiple proteincoding DNA alignments. Biochem. Biophys. Res. Commun. 419, 779â781 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#ref-CR98" id="ref-link-section-d58070e2653">98</a></sup>) with the YangâNielsen approach implemented in PAML<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 99" title="Yang, Z. PAML 4: phylogenetic analysis by maximum likelihood. Mol. Biol. Evol. 24, 1586â1591 (2007)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#ref-CR99" id="ref-link-section-d58070e2657">99</a></sup>. The Ks distribution plot (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#MOESM1">27</a>) was drawn using in-house Python and R scripts.</p><p>Ancient whole-genome duplication (WGD), also known as paleopolyploidization events, is shared throughout angiosperm history<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 100" title="Van de Peer, Y. A mystery unveiled. Genome Biol. 12, 113 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#ref-CR100" id="ref-link-section-d58070e2667">100</a></sup> and represents a powerful evolutionary force for diversification, neofunctionalization, and innovation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Freeling, M. &amp; Thomas, B. C. Gene-balanced duplications, like tetraploidy, provide predictable drive to increase morphological complexity. Genome Res. 16, 805â814 (2006)." href="#ref-CR101" id="ref-link-section-d58070e2671">101</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Otto, S. P. The evolutionary consequences of polyploidy. Cell 131, 452â462 (2007)." href="#ref-CR102" id="ref-link-section-d58070e2671_1">102</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 103" title="Schranz, M. E., Mohammadin, S. &amp; Edger, P. P. Ancient whole genome duplications, novelty and diversification: the WGD radiation lag-time model. Curr. Opin. Plant Biol. 15, 147â153 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#ref-CR103" id="ref-link-section-d58070e2674">103</a></sup>. We did not detect the peak of the recent WGD found in soybean, suggesting that Caesalpinoideae, including <i>S. tora</i> and <i>Mimosa pudica,</i> do not have the soybean-specific WGD event (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#MOESM1">27</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 104" title="Schmutz, J. et al. Genome sequence of the palaeopolyploid soybean. Nature 463, 178â183 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#ref-CR104" id="ref-link-section-d58070e2688">104</a></sup>. Homology analysis with 6,310 orthologous genes shared by <i>S. tora</i> and 15 other green plant species was used to construct a phylogenetic tree based on a concatenated sequence alignment using MAFFT v7.305b. In this phylogenetic tree, <i>S. tora</i>, as expected, clustered with other legume crops, although the evolutionary distance from <i>S. tora</i> to Papilionoideae such as soybean, <i>Medicago truncatula</i>, and chickpea was relatively large (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#MOESM1">11</a>). The phylogenetic tree confirmed the grouping of Caesalpinioideae species such as <i>S. tora</i> and <i>M. pudica</i>. The first divergence between Caesalpinioideae and Papilionoideae was estimated at approximately 81.9â93.6 MYA (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#MOESM1">11</a>). Furthermore, <i>Senna</i> and <i>Chamaecrista</i> genera diverged from the Mimosoid clade (<i>Faidherbia albida</i> and <i>Mimosa pudica</i>) ~59.4â66.5 MYA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 105" title="Bruneau, A., Mercure, M., Lewis, G. P. &amp; Herendeen, P. S. Phylogenetic patterns and diversification in the caesalpinioid legumes. Botany 86, 697â718 (2008)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#ref-CR105" id="ref-link-section-d58070e2730">105</a></sup> (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#MOESM1">11</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec20">Enzyme prediction</h3><p><i>S. tora</i> enzymes and metabolic pathways were predicted using the Plant Metabolic Network (PMN)âs pipelines<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 106" title="SchlÃ¤pfer, P. et al. Genome-wide prediction of metabolic enzymes, pathways, and gene clusters in plants. Plant Physiol. 173, 2041â2059 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#ref-CR106" id="ref-link-section-d58070e2747">106</a></sup>. This process starts by annotating amino acid sequences with the Ensemble Enzyme Prediction Pipeline (E2P2) version 4.0 to identify enzymes and assign reactions they may catalyze based on a database of known enzymes called Reference Protein Sequence Database (RPSD) version 4.2. Metabolic pathways were predicted using PathoLogic software, which is part of the Pathway Tools v23.5 package from SRI<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 107" title="Karp, P. D. et al. Pathway tools version 23.0 update: software for pathway/genome informatics and systems biology. Brief. Bioinform. 2019, bbz104 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#ref-CR107" id="ref-link-section-d58070e2751">107</a></sup>. The predicted pathways were further refined using PMNâs Semi-Automated Validation Infrastructure (SAVI) software version 3.1 by applying pathway assignment criteria based on manual curations. In total, 6,159 enzymes and 442 metabolic pathways were predicted in <i>S. tora</i> into a database called StoraCyc, available online at <a href="https://plantcyc.org/">https://plantcyc.org/</a>.</p><p>To identify enriched metabolic pathways among expanded or rapidly expanded gene families, we created the following four datasets: Dataset 1 included 15,921 genes from 2,874 families that were expanded or rapidly expanded in <i>S. tora</i>. Dataset 2 included 10,571 genes from 1,775 families that were exclusively expanded or rapidly expanded in <i>S. tora</i>. Dataset 3 included 7,382 genes from 411 families that were rapidly expanded in <i>S. tora</i>. Dataset 4 included 5,693 genes from 306 families that were exclusively rapidly expanded in <i>S. tora</i> (Supplementary Data<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#MOESM7">3</a>). The background used in this enrichment analysis was all genes in <i>S. tora</i>. P values of the enrichment analysis were calculated with a hypergeometric test using the phyper() function followed by multiple test correction using false-discovery rate (FDR) via <i>P</i>.adjust(), both functions from the stats package version 3.6.2 in R version 3.6.3. Significant enrichment was defined as an adjusted <i>P</i> value â¤â0.01.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec21">Primary metabolite profiling</h3><p>Metabolome analysis was performed with 21 samples of frozen seed powders (~50âmg each) collected from seven seed developmental stages using the Capillary Electrophoresis Time of Flight Mass Spectrometry (CE-TOF-MS). CE-TOF-MS was run in two modes for cationic and anionic metabolites at Human Metabolome Technologies (Yamagata, Japan). The samples were mixed with 500âÎ¼L of methanol containing internal standards (50âÎ¼M) and homogenized using a homogenizer (a cell-breakage machine with beads (MS-100R, TOMY Digital Biology, Tokyo, Japan)). Then, chloroform (500âÎ¼L) and Milli-Q water (200âÎ¼L) were added to the homogenates, mixed thoroughly, and centrifuged (2300âÃâ<i>g</i>, 4âÂ°C, 5âmin). The water layer (200âÎ¼L) was filtrated twice through 5-kDa cut-off filter (Ultra-free MC-PLHCC, Human Metabolome Technologies, Yamagata, Japan) to remove macromolecules. The filtrate was centrifuged and resuspended in 50âÎ¼L of ultrapure water immediately before the measurement. Cationic metabolite levels were analyzed using a commercial fused silica capillary (H3305-1002, HMT; i.d. 50âÎ¼MâÃâ80âcm) with a commercial cationic electrophoresis buffer (H3301â1001, HMT), or anionic electrophoresis buffer (H3301â1020, HMT) as the electrolyte. A commercial sheath liquid (H3301â1020, HMT) was delivered at a rate of 10âÎ¼L/min. Approximately 10ânL of sample solution was injected at a pressure of 50âmbar for 10âs, and applied capillary voltages were set at 27âkV (cation mode) and 30âkV (anion mode), respectively. For both cationic and anionic modes, the spectrometer was scanned from <i>m/z</i> 50 to 1,000.</p><p>Peaks detected in CE-TOF-MS were extracted using an automated integration software (MasterHands ver. 2.16.0.15 developed at Keio University) in order to obtain peak information including <i>m/z</i>, migration time (MT), and peak area. The peak detection limit was set at the signal-noise ratio (S/N) of 3. Signal peaks corresponding to isotopomers, adduct ions, and other product ions of known metabolites were excluded, and the remaining peaks were annotated with putative metabolites from the MasterHands database based on their MTs and <i>m/z</i> values. The tolerance range for the peak annotation was configured at Â±0.5âmin for MT and Â±10 ppm for <i>m/z</i>. For the 178 peaks detected (Supplementary DataÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#MOESM9">5</a>), the average relative area and standard deviations (S.D.) were calculated in the 7 developmental stages of <i>S. tora</i> seeds. Absolute quantification was performed for 110 metabolites, including glycolytic and TCA-cycle intermediates, amino acids, and nucleic acids. All the metabolite concentrations were calculated by normalizing the peak area of each metabolite with respect to the area of the internal standard (solution ID: H3304-1002, HMT, Inc.) and by using standard curves, which were obtained by single-point (100âÎ¼M) calibrations. Finally, we obtained absolute quantitative values for 69 out of 110 metabolites (Supplementary DataÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#MOESM10">6</a>). The ratio of the average relative peak area and <i>P</i> value from Welchâs <i>t</i> tests were calculated between the two stages (stage 1 vs. other stages).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec22">Anthraquinone extraction and analysis</h3><p><i>S. tora</i> seeds were collected and sorted into seven different ripening stages (Stage 1âStage 7) depending on their size, color, and hardness. Classified seeds were ground with a mortar and pestle using liquid nitrogen to a fine powder and freeze-dried. Powdered samples (20âmg) were extracted with 1âmL of methanol using sonication for 30âmin at 60âÂ°C. After extraction, samples were centrifuged at 500âÃâ<i>g</i> for 3âmin at 25âÂ°C, and the supernatant was filtered with 0.2âÎ¼M Acrodisc MS syringe filters with PTFE membrane (Pall Corporation, Port Washington, NY, USA). The filtrate was completely dried by EvaT-0200 Total Concentration System equipped with EvaS-3600 N2 generator (Goojung engineering, Seoul, Korea), mixed with methanol, and filtered again with Acrodisc 0.2âÎ¼M MS syringe filter for liquid chromatographyâmass spectrometry (LCâMS) analysis.</p><p>Quantitative analysis of anthraquinones was performed by a 3200 QTRAP mass spectrometer with a Turbo V ion source (AB Sciex, Ontario, CA, USA) coupled with a VANQUISH UHPLC system (Thermo Fisher Scientific, CA, USA) equipped with binary solvent manager, sample manager, column heater, and photodiode array detector. UHPLC was performed on a ZORBAX Eclipse Plus column (1.8âÎ¼M, 2.1âmmâÃâ100âmm, Agilent Technology, CA, USA) and mobile phases consisted of 5âmM ammonium acetate in water (eluent A) and 100% acetonitrile (eluent B). The gradient conditions were as follows: 0â1âmin, 10% B; 1â4.5âmin, 10â30% B; 4.5â8âmin, 30â50% B; 8â11âmin, 50â100% B; 11â14âmin, 100% B. The flow rate was 0.5âmL/min and two microliters of samples were injected. For detecting peaks from test samples, MS parameter in ESI-negative mode was used as follows: nebulizing gas, 50 psi; heating gas, 50 psi; curtain gas, 20 psi; desolvation temperature, 550âÂ°C; ion-spray voltage floating, 4.5âkV. The data obtained from MRM mode were quantitated using MultiQuant 3.0.2 software (AB SCIEX).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec23">RNA sequencing and analysis</h3><p>Total RNA was isolated from seven developmental stages of seeds (Stage 1âStage 7) (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#MOESM1">20</a>). RNA extraction and RNA-Seq library preparations were performed, and RNA-Seq libraries were sequenced on the Illumina NextSeq 500 (Illumina, San Diego, USA). First, low-quality bases (PHERD score (Q)â&lt;â20) and adaptor contamination were removed by Trimmomatic v0.36 using the parameters âILLUMINACLIP:TruSeq3-SE:2:30:10 LEADING:3 SLIDINGWINDOW:4:15 MINLEN:36â<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Manoharan, R. K., Lee, J.-H., Kim, Y.-G. &amp; Lee, J. Alizarin and chrysazin inhibit biofilm and hyphal formation by Candida albicans. Front. Cell Infect. Microbiol. 7, 447â447 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d58070e2862">2</a></sup>. After checking for quality scores and read lengths, RNA-Seq reads were mapped <i>to S. tora</i> genome using STAR-2.6.0a with default parameters<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 108" title="Dobin, A. et al. STAR: ultrafast universal RNA-seq aligner. Bioinformatics 29, 15â21 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#ref-CR108" id="ref-link-section-d58070e2869">108</a></sup>. Expectation Maximization (RSEM-1.3.1)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 109" title="Li, B. &amp; Dewey, C. N. RSEM: accurate transcript quantification from RNA-Seq data with or without a reference genome. BMC BioinformaticsÂ 12, 323 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#ref-CR109" id="ref-link-section-d58070e2873">109</a></sup> method was used to obtain the expression value for each gene in the genome (Supplementary DataÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#MOESM13">9</a>). The read counts estimated by RSEM were subjected to edgeR v3.22.5Â (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 110" title="Robinson, M. D., McCarthy, D. J. &amp; Smyth, G. K. edgeR: a Bioconductor package for differential expression analysis of digital gene expression data. Bioinformatics 26, 139â140 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#ref-CR110" id="ref-link-section-d58070e2881">110</a></sup>) to obtain differential expression scores along with the statistical significance based on the FDR. Furthermore, we applied the standard filters, i.e., genes per million (TPM)ââ¥â0.3, read counts â¥â5, and log2-fold changes â¥â1 orââ¤ââ1 to derive the final list of differentially expressed genes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 111" title="Kim, D. et al. Transcriptional profiles of secondary metabolite biosynthesis genes and cytochromes in the leaves of four papaver species. Data 3, 55 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#ref-CR111" id="ref-link-section-d58070e2885">111</a></sup>. Finally, the expressed genes (i.e., TPMââ¥â0.3 and read count â¥â5) were included to show the different expression patterns during seed development. An in-house R script was used to generate the heatmap.</p><p>To analyze gene expression patterns of metabolic genes during seed development, all expressed (TPMâ&gt;â0) <i>S. tora</i> genes predicted to catalyze small-molecule metabolism in StoraCyc were mapped to StoraCycâs metabolic domains (Supplementary DataÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#MOESM8">4</a>) based on their associated reactions. A violin plot was generated using ggplot2 version 3.3.2 in R version 3.6.3 to show the expression patterns of genes belonging to metabolic domains during seed development (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#MOESM1">15</a> and Supplementary DataÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#MOESM8">4</a>).</p><p>The genes determined as DEGs in at least one pair of comparisons were selected, resulting in a total of 13,488 genes. The mean across replicates with base 2 of logarithm was clustered by <i>k</i>-means (<i>k</i>â=â9) using the standard function of <i>k</i>-means in R version 3.6.3 with default parameters. Core genes were identified using a cluster score and overlaid on the plot representing expression values with ggplot2 version 3.3.2 in R version 3.6.3.</p><p>To identify enriched StoraCyc metabolic domains in clusters 3 and 6, we compared the metabolic domain annotations of genes annotated to these clusters to those annotated to all genes in <i>S. tora</i>. P values of the enrichment analysis were calculated with a hypergeometric test using phyper()Â followed by multiple test correction using False Discovery Rate via <i>P</i>.adjust(), both functions from the stats package version 3.6.2 in R version 3.6.3. Significant enrichment was defined as an adjusted <i>P</i> valueââ¤â0.01. To avoid biases introduced by datasets containing a small number of genes, we defined a minimum threshold of at least ten genes present in each metabolic domain per cluster in order for the domain to be considered significantly enriched. The bubble plot was generated using ggplot2 version 3.3.2 in R version 3.6.3.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec24">Heterologous protein expression and enzyme assays</h3><p>STO07G228250 (1,173 bp) encoding CHS-L9 and STO03G058250 (1,173 bp) encoding CHS cDNAs were PCR-amplified using a pair of oligonucleotide primers (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#MOESM1">21</a>). STO07G228250 and STO03G058250 were cloned in pET28a(+) vector. The <i>E. coli</i> BL21 (DE3) strain harboring correct pET28a(+)_STO07G228250 and pET28a(+)_STO03G058250 plasmids was used for protein production. Cultures were induced by 0.4âmM isopropyl-Î²-D-thiogalactopyranoside (GeneChem, Daejeon, Korea) to start the recombinant protein expression. After incubation at 20âÂ°C for 20â24âh, the cells were harvested by centrifugation, washed twice with 100âmM phosphate buffer saline (pH 7.5) containing 10% glycerol, and disrupted by sonication. The homogenates were centrifuged at 13,475âÃâ<i>g</i> for 30âmin at 4âÂ°C to isolate soluble proteins from insoluble cell debris. The supernatants were applied to a separate column containing 1âml of His<sub>6</sub> Ni-Superflow Resin (Takara, Japan), which was equilibrated with a buffer containing 100âmM phosphate buffer saline (pH 7.5), 500âmM NaCl, 5âmM imidazole, 1âmM dithiothreitol, and 10% glycerol. The His<sub>6</sub>-tagged recombinant proteins were then eluted with eight column volumes of the aforementioned buffer containing 50âmM of imidazole. The elution was repeated with the same buffer containing 250âmM of imidazole. The purity and molecular mass of the recombinant proteins were verified by 12% SDS-PAGE. The fractions containing the pure protein were then pooled and concentrated using Amicon Ultra 15 (Millipore, 30âK NMWL centrifugal filters). Protein concentrations were measured by the Bradford method using the Bradford reagent (Protein Assay Dc, Bio-Rad, Hercules, CA, USA) using bovine serum albumin as standard.</p><p>Enzyme assays for anthraquinone biosynthesis were carried out in 1âml volume in a microcentrifuge tube containing 5âmM of malonyl-CoA (Sigma-Aldrich, St. Louis, USA), 10âmM of MgCl<sub>2</sub>, and 10âÂµg/ml of pure protein in 100âmM phosphate buffer saline (pH 7.5). An identical reaction mixture containing the same amount of heat-denatured protein served as a negative control. A separate reaction was carried out with the same reaction constituents with an additional 1âmM of NADPH as a cofactor. Similarly, assays were carried out with identical reaction components, except for malonyl-CoA, which was replaced with the same amount of <sup>13</sup>C<sub>3</sub>-malonyl-CoA (Sigma-Aldrich, St. Louis, USA). All reaction mixtures were incubated at 30âÂ°C for 6âh, and stopped by heating the reaction mixture at 85âÂ°C for 3âmin.</p><p>For theÂ STO03G058250 (CHS) enzyme, separate sets of reactions were carried out in the presence of <i>p</i>-coumaroyl-CoA (PlantMetaChem, Giessen, Germany) as the starting substrate and malonyl-CoA and <sup>13</sup>C<sub>3</sub>-malonyl-CoA as extender substrates. Each reaction mixture contained 2âmM of <i>p</i>-coumaroyl-CoA, 5âmM of extender substrates, 10âmM of MgCl<sub>2</sub>, and 10âÂµg/ml of pure protein in 100âmM phosphate buffer saline (pH 7.5). An identical reaction mixture without the starting substrate served as a negative control. Each reaction was performed in three biological replicates.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec25">Enzyme assay quantification</h3><p>The quenched reaction mixtures were centrifuged at 13,475âÃâ<i>g</i> for 30âmin to separate denatured protein, filtered through 0.2-Âµm syringe filter, and subjected to reverse-phase ultrapressure liquid chromatography (RPUPLC) coupled with photodiode array (PDA) when necessary, followed by high-resolution time-of-flight electrospray ionization (HRTOF ESI-MS) analysis. All enzyme assays were performed in triplicates.</p><p>RPUPLC-PDA was performed with an RP-18 column (50-mm long, 2.1-mm internal diameter, and 1.7-Âµm particle size) in Acquity (Waters) with UPLC LG 500Â nm PDA detector using water as aqueous solvent A and acetonitrile (Thermo Fisher Scientific Korea, Seoul, Korea) as organic solvent B at the flow rate of 0.3âmL/min for 12âmin under the following conditions of solvent B (0â100%) for (0â7) min, 100% for (7â9.5) min, and 0% for (9.6â12) min. HRQTOF ESI-MS and ESI-MS<sup>2</sup> were performed in Acquity SYNAPT G2-S mass spectrometer (Waters, Milford, MA, USA). The selected precursor ions were further subjected to TOF-ESI-MS<sup>2</sup> analysis in positive ionization mode.</p><p>The CHS enzyme STO03G058250 was investigated for its possible involvement in flavonoid biosynthesis. The reaction of STO03G058250 with <i>p</i>-coumaroyl-CoA and malonyl-CoA generated naringenin chalcone along with bisnoryangonin, and <i>p</i>-coumaroyltriacetic acid lactone (CTAL) demonstrating its participation in flavonoid biosynthesis in <i>S. tora</i> (Supplementary Figs.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#MOESM1">28</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#MOESM1">29</a>). The pyrone ring containing metabolites, bisnoryangonin and CTAL, are the shunt products produced after two and three malonyl-CoA condensations, respectively<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 112" title="Pluskal, T. et al. The biosynthetic origin of psychoactive kavalactones in kava. Nat. Plants 5, 867â878 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#ref-CR112" id="ref-link-section-d58070e3011">112</a></sup>. None of the stilbene-type derivatives were produced in the reactions.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec26">Reporting summary</h3><p>Further information on research design is available in theÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#MOESM3">Nature Research Reporting Summary</a> linked to this article.</p></div></div></section>

              <p>Data supporting the findings of this work are available within the paper and its Supplementary Information files. A reporting summary for this article is available as a Supplementary Information file. The datasets and plant materials generated and analyzed during the current study are available from the corresponding author upon request. Genome sequence reads, transcriptome sequence reads, Hi-C sequence reads, GBS sequence reads, and BAC library sequence data are deposited in GenBank under project number <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/PRJNA605066">PRJNA605066</a>. The genome assemblies and annotation files are deposited in GenBank under accession number <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/JAAIUW000000000">JAAIUW000000000</a>. The <i>S. tora</i> genome is also available at <a href="http://nabic.rda.go.kr/Species/Senna_tora2">http://nabic.rda.go.kr/Species/Senna_tora2</a>.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="section anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-19681-1#Sec28">Source data</a> are provided with this paper.</p>

              <p>In-house Python and R scripts for gene prediction, Ks distribution plot, and heatmap analyses can be freely downloaded at GitHub [<a href="https://github.com/MyungheeJung/Senna_tora.git">https://github.com/MyungheeJung/Senna_tora.git</a>].</p>

                <p>S.H.K., J.K.S., and R.P.P. have filed a patent application (application number: 10-2020-0075168) on the gene function of a chalcone synthase-like (CHS-L) protein-encoding ORF (STO07G228250) discovered in this study.
